# DOCUMENT 1: Clear Current Technologies — Master Research Synthesis (Primary + Secondary)

*Source file: Clear Current Technologies — Master Research Synthesis.md*

---

# Clear Current Technologies
## Master Research Synthesis: Primary + Secondary Evidence Brief

**Prepared for:** Dan Schreiber (CPO) & John Reuter (CEO)
**Team:** Clayton Hester | Jason Wisnieski | Adam Case | Victor Pan
**CDL Texas MBA Consulting | April 2026**
**Version:** 2.0 — Updated to incorporate all 16 primary interviews

---

This brief consolidates the full body of primary research conducted for Clear Current Technologies — 16 interviews across higher education, healthcare, QSR, hospitality, channel partners, and market advisors — and maps each finding to the secondary research assembled across six deep research sessions, three university databases (IBISWorld, ABI/Inform, Orbis), and a full claim verification audit. The goal is to show Dan and John where primary and secondary sources converge, where they diverge, and where gaps remain.

**16** Interviews | **6** Research Sessions | **3** Database Sources | **12+** Stats Verified | **5** Deliverables

---

## 02 — Primary Research: Who We Spoke To and Why It Matters

---

### Higher Education (3 institutions, 5 interviewees)

**Texas State University** — Andee Chamberlain, Carl Teague, James Norton. Active EnergyCAP users managing ~1,500 meters across 98 buildings in a district energy model with central steam and chilled water plants. This interview produced the richest on-the-ground EnergyCAP critique in the research program, confirmed the district energy metering problem, and validated the finance/ops dual-audience tension from the operations side.

**UT Austin — Finance** — Andi Ault, Sr. Associate Director for Finance. Finance-side perspective at a PEER Platinum district energy system with ~$20M annual gas spend. Confirmed the dual-audience problem from the finance chair. Identified the budget timing gap (budget locked in March before Austin finalizes rates) as the clearest real-world use case for regulatory intelligence.

**UT Austin — Engineering** — Troy Nevells, Director of Engineering & Technical Services. Production-side engineering perspective: UT generates ~100% of its own electricity via combined cycle, making external utility invoice volume low. Troy immediately redirected the value proposition toward multi-site commercial operators and QSR — specifically naming Panda — as the highest-fit market, validating Clear Current's beachhead thesis from an insider who understands the billing problem deeply.

### Healthcare (5 systems, 8 interviewees)

**AdventHealth** — John Culver (Sustainability) & Scott Sukits (Director, Control Systems & Energy Management, Central Florida). 55 hospitals across 9 states; 73 different electric utilities; 1,000–2,000 utility bills processed daily. Surfaced the "ghost meter / address reconciliation" problem as the #1 automation wish. BrainBox AI via Trane stuck in IT/cybersecurity review for 2.5+ years — the clearest evidence of the IT security gate problem outside of Trane's own testimony.

**Bon Secours Mercy Health** — Ann Walston, VP Acute Care Facilities Management. 36 fully owned hospitals; all electricity and gas purchased from the grid; Utilities Procurement Steering Committee sets hedging strategy quarterly. Provided the most detailed account of a major billing error recovery in the entire research program: a billing error totaling $5–6M in a single event, with only $1M ultimately recovered — a partial, board-level outcome that required months of utility dispute and illustrated the "identified vs. realized" gap in the clearest terms of any interview. Uses third-party processor Get Choice; Energy Star as the primary benchmarking framework. Finance posture is reactive except for hedging; rate trend visibility would be the second proactive lever her team currently lacks.

**Intermountain Health** — Ross Snow (Director of Energy Management), Matt Wilson, Bart Peacock. 14-person energy management team — the largest internal hospital energy team encountered in this research program. 14 billion BAS data points in SkySpark. Third party missed a bill spike from ~$40K to ~$140K; internal accountant caught it. Described the two-layer model (optimization vs. capital replacement on opposite seasonal schedules) that is the most important operational calendar framework for healthcare.

**Ascension / Medxcel** — Scott Czubkowski, PE, CHC, CEM. Formerly managed $265M annual utility spend for Ascension; now at Medxcel. Built a three-team supply side / engineering / facilities performance structure. Transitioned to Peer AI (Constellation Energy) nine months prior. Provided the most sophisticated real-world AI use cases: dead meter detection ($32/month exact charge identified as inactive), polar vortex gas nomination alerting (single event cost $7.2M; proactive nomination estimated to be worth several million annually), and hospital-to-hospital benchmarking by square footage and climate zone.

**Houston Methodist** — Sean Sevy, Director of Operations, System Facilities. 8 hospitals + 300+ outpatient locations across Houston. Provided the best 35-year historical arc of energy management evolution (pneumatic → DDC → BAS → AI). Uses Energy Print for billing and Energy Star; Prism Energy as broker. Candidly assessed that a unified billing-plus-operations platform is likely 3–5 years from widespread adoption, but described Clear Current's current positioning as exactly right for the billing-first wedge.

### QSR (1 company, 2 interviewees with distinct vantage points)

**Roger Goldstein, Panda Restaurant Group** — Executive Director, Facilities & Energy. Internal perspective on a 2,300-location chain outsourcing bill processing to NG Insight (third party). Produced the clearest real-world validation of the "baseline drift" problem: Panda paid a neighbor's electricity for an entire year because the charge was consistent enough to establish a new baseline — never flagged by NG Insight's threshold rules.

**Walt Taylor, GWT2Energy (Panda contractor)** — External energy consultant with unusual candor. One of the few interviewees with hands-on PSC rate case experience (successfully argued a utility's calculation methodology was inequitable). Confirmed the ENGIE Impact / Arcadia relationship, which has direct implications for the data access problem in the QSR vertical. No AI in use anywhere in Panda's current energy management process.

### Hospitality (1 company, 1 interviewee)

**Randy Dawes, Hyatt Hotels Corporation** — Director, Openings Engineering. Hyatt's owner-operator structure means corporate does not touch utility invoices — billing responsibility falls to individual property owners, asset managers, and management companies. The right buyer in hospitality is not a brand's corporate team. Surfaced metrics fragmentation (different units across properties) and invoice automation (late arrivals causing late fees) as the two most clearly articulated pain points.

### Channel Partners (2 interviewees)

**Don Johnson, Trane Technologies** — CEM with 9 years at El Paso Electric and 20 years on the solutions side. Dual-lens perspective on how utilities construct bills and how commercial customers misunderstand them. Validated the "fake energy manager" persona, the reactive buyer pattern, and billing as the entry point into the relationship.

**Phil Combs, Trane Technologies** — Sr. Digital Services Engineer, CMVP. Described Excel as the primary tool for daily energy analysis, even within Trane's own technology portfolio. Identified regulatory intelligence as the "killer feature nobody has" — the strongest language used about any feature in the entire research program. Identified bill format variation as the core technical barrier to previous AI energy tools.

### Strategic Advisors & Ecosystem (3 interviewees)

**Marc Spieler, NVIDIA** — Sr. MD, Global Energy Industry; Board of EPRI. Identified three distinct GTM tracks (direct enterprise, sell to utilities, license to ERP platforms). Named Arcadia as the primary AI-native competitive threat. Proposed the property tax protest model as the right pricing analogy — evergreen % of savings, zero fee if nothing found. Described the identified-vs-realized error problem (recovered credits often go uncollected).

**Jamare Bates, JLL** — VP, Clean Energy & Infrastructure Advisory. Provided the most expansive macro framing: energy is becoming the #1 site selection factor, legacy buildings are now financial liabilities, and the office vacancy crisis compounds the energy inefficiency problem across CRE. Works with Fortune 200/500 clients and public sector / higher ed institutions.

**Adam Zavadsky, ENGIE Impact** — Senior Account Manager. Described ENGIE Impact's competitive moat as the data itself — 20 years of client utility data in their system — not the dashboard or analytics. Confirmed that ENGIE Impact uses AI primarily for internal bill processing efficiency, not client-facing analytics. Identified the structural data access problem: a new platform entering a relationship where ENGIE already holds all the historical data must either partner with ENGIE or displace them entirely.

---

## 03 — Primary + Secondary Correlations: Where All 16 Interviews Map

The table below shows where primary interview findings are confirmed, quantified, or expanded by secondary research. "STRONG" means both streams agree with specific evidence from multiple interviews. "MODERATE" means directional agreement but one stream is weaker or coverage is partial. "EMERGING" means primary evidence is building but secondary data is limited.

---

### Finding 1: The Reactive Buyer Is the Norm, Not the Exception

**Primary Evidence (11 interviews):** Don Johnson (Trane): "Most customers are still reactive — even hospitals." Phil Combs: "Tyranny of the urgent." Roger Goldstein (Panda): exception-only monitoring — if a $2,000 average bill jumps to $12,000, it gets flagged; otherwise, not. Walt Taylor (Panda): "management by exception — look at the top 20% of outliers and investigate those." Adam Zavadsky (ENGIE Impact): most clients reactive; ENGIE's value prop is labor avoidance, not proactive optimization. Ann Walston (Bon Secours): reactive except for hedging, which is the one proactive lever. Andi Ault (UT Austin): finance is reactive; hedging is the one proactive tool. Scott Sukits (AdventHealth): described wanting to get ahead of problems, not just respond to them. Sean Sevy (Houston Methodist): historically reactive; beginning to incorporate proactive BAS analytics. Jamare Bates (JLL): most clients arriving only after energy costs have already become a crisis.

**Secondary Corroboration:** EnergyCAP State of Utilities 2026: 76% of commercial energy buyers expect utility costs to rise but 69% remain reactive on cost reduction. ABI/Inform: 30%+ of facility managers report a deferred-maintenance mindset. Verdantix 2023: all 15 leading EMS vendors incorporating AI because buyers want automation over manual workflows.

Six-Thread Secondary Brief: fewer than 30% of multi-site commercial operators have a named, dedicated Energy Manager — meaning the reactive buyer is often also a part-time buyer, juggling energy alongside facilities, operations, or finance. At a 200-site operator, this translates to 400–1,000+ monthly invoices landing with no dedicated reviewer. Top 7 documented buying triggers (ranked by frequency in secondary research): (1) a large, unexpected bill; (2) a CFO-level cost reduction mandate; (3) sustainability/ESG reporting requirement; (4) a new hire or external consultant identifying a gap; (5) a competitor adoption signal; (6) a regulatory compliance deadline; (7) a utility rate increase announcement. The fully loaded cost of a dedicated Energy Manager is $120K–$160K salary plus benefits, or $200K+ total — creating a strong internal incentive to delay hiring and tolerate reactive management. FTE benchmarks from secondary research: best-in-class operators run 200–500 locations per energy FTE; average commercial operators run far higher ratios with no dedicated FTE at all.

**Deliverable Link:** Engagement Trigger Map; User Journey Maps; Product Module Recommendations (push dashboard is not optional — the user will not go looking for what's wrong; it has to find them)

**Strength: STRONG**

---

### Finding 2: The Dual-Audience Problem Is Structural and Unsolved

**Primary Evidence (6 interviews):** Andee Chamberlain (Texas State): "I care more about demand and usage — the bill processing folks care more about the bottom line." Andi Ault (UT Austin): confirmed from the finance side — when asked whether a cross-collaborative tool made sense, her immediate reaction was that the two audiences have completely different questions. Ann Walston (Bon Secours): finance-side focus on cost predictability, not operational efficiency; hedging as the primary lever. Scott Czubkowski (Ascension): described three structurally separated teams — supply side, engineering, and facilities performance — each with different data needs from the same underlying energy spend. Troy Nevells (UT Austin): engineering production lens vs. finance billing lens — same campus, completely different data relationships. John Culver & Scott Sukits (AdventHealth): sustainability reporting vs. operations optimization — John asks "are we on track for our 2030 goals?"; Scott asks "why is this building using 20% more than last month?"

**Secondary Corroboration:** EnergyCAP user reviews (G2/Capterra, 132 total): system described as not serving both audiences well. No vendor in the competitive landscape offers a single platform with separate views for energy operators and finance users confirmed in secondary competitive research.

**Deliverable Link:** User Journey Maps; Product Module Recommendations (chat for expert, push dashboard for finance — Dan's architecture is validated by six independent interviews)

**Strength: STRONG**

---

### Finding 3: Billing Errors Are Real, Material, and Structurally Persistent

**Primary Evidence (7 interviews):** Roger Goldstein (Panda): paid a neighbor's electricity for an entire year — consistent enough to establish a new baseline, never flagged by NG Insight's threshold system. This is the clearest real-world validation of the "baseline drift" problem. Ann Walston (Bon Secours): over her career she has encountered billing errors totaling $5–6M in a single event, with $1M ultimately recovered — a partial recovery that required months of utility dispute, reached board-level visibility, and illustrated how identified errors rarely convert fully to realized credits. Texas State (Andee Chamberlain): "We had a weird multiplier happen in one of our bills — they listed 35,000 gallons instead of 3,500, a 10× error in a pattern that should have been obvious." Intermountain Health (Ross Snow): a third party should have flagged a bill that spiked from ~$40K to ~$140K — they didn't; an internal accountant caught it. Scott Czubkowski (Ascension): Peer AI identified a dead meter paying exactly $32/month for months — a charge so consistent it never triggered threshold alerts under the previous system. Across Czubkowski's portfolio, ghost meters and vacated-site billings accumulated to more than $1M/year in payments for locations no longer drawing power — a recurring, systematic error class that threshold-based systems cannot detect. Walt Taylor (Panda): ENGIE Impact flags outliers but cannot catch baseline drift; the management-by-exception approach has a structural blind spot for systematic errors. Don Johnson (Trane): 1 in 5 utility bills contain some form of error — framing consistent with EnergyCAP's own marketing claims.

**Secondary Corroboration:** FERC audit: $108M mischarged at a single utility (FirstEnergy). Rhode Island PUC: 83% of internally flagged billing exceptions were sent to customers anyway. Harvard Law (2025): structural information asymmetry perpetuates billing errors. EnergyCAP: claims 1 in 5 bills contain errors; 98% of users take monthly action on flagged bills.

Six-Thread Secondary Brief adds three material data points: (1) UtiliSave, a specialist utility bill auditing firm, reports a 94% audit-hit rate — meaning 94% of commercial accounts they audit contain at least one correctable billing error — and has recovered more than $650 million in overcharges for clients since 1991. (2) ENGIE Impact internal estimates put the error rate in commercial utility bills at approximately 17%, meaning roughly 1 in 6 bills has a recoverable error — a more conservative but still significant figure than EnergyCAP's 1-in-5 claim, and from a credible competitor source. (3) Demand charges — the peak-demand component of a commercial electricity bill — represent 30–70% of total commercial electricity cost and are the single most opaque and most frequently miscalculated line item on a commercial utility bill; this is the structural reason billing errors are so persistent and so material.

**Note on the "80% overpaying" statistic:** This claim cannot be used — it is vendor-generated and fails the claim verification audit. The government-source framing (FERC, RI PUC, Harvard Law) and the UtiliSave/ENGIE Impact competitor data are the credible substitutes.

**Deliverable Link:** Product Module Recommendations; Engagement Trigger Map; Opportunity Heatmap

**Strength: STRONG**

---

### Finding 4: Threshold-Based Exception Rules Have a Structural Blind Spot (New — Not in Original Synthesis)

**Primary Evidence (4 interviews):** Roger Goldstein (Panda): NG Insight's threshold rules work for spikes but miss gradual or systematic errors that establish a new baseline. Panda paid a neighboring tenant's electricity for a year because the charge was consistent, not anomalous. Scott Czubkowski (Ascension): Peer AI's dead meter detection ($32/month exact, month after month) required pattern-matching across time — not threshold alerting. Walt Taylor (Panda): "management by exception" catches the top 20% of outliers but is blind to systematic errors that blend into the average. Ann Walston (Bon Secours): described meter failure events where the charge was wrong but not obviously wrong — the error was structural, not a spike.

**Secondary Corroboration:** Rhode Island PUC: 83% of flagged exceptions sent to customers anyway (system-level failure, not just individual errors). No secondary source specifically addresses the baseline drift problem — this is primarily a primary research finding.

**Deliverable Link:** Product Module Recommendations (anomaly detection must be framed as "pattern deviation detection," not "spike detection")

**Strength: STRONG (primary) / EMERGING (secondary)**

---

### Finding 5: Regulatory Intelligence Is Confirmed White Space With Documented Business Cases

**Primary Evidence (5 interviews):** Phil Combs (Trane): "super powerful" — called regulatory intelligence stronger than any other feature discussed; described monitoring PUC filings, utility board meetings, rate hike proposals, and grid operator decisions as a capability no one currently offers. Andi Ault (UT Austin): budget locked in March before City of Austin finalizes rates for the July 1 fiscal year — the exact regulatory timing gap this feature solves. Walt Taylor (Panda): one of the few interviewees with hands-on PSC rate case experience — successfully argued a utility's calculation methodology was inequitable by making a quantitative case before the state commission. Ann Walston (Bon Secours): hedging is the only proactive lever available; forward-looking rate intelligence would give her a second lever. Sean Sevy (Houston Methodist): no current regulatory monitoring capability; described it as a gap he is aware of.

**Secondary Corroboration:** Ohio PUC mandates 275-day rate case timelines; AES Ohio 2024 rate case resulted in ~9% commercial rate increase with minimal advance notice. PPL Pennsylvania case filed October 2025 with final decision July 1, 2026 — the same day a university's new fiscal year begins. Boston University locks budget in March before final rates are known. Commercial energy CAGR is 5.9% vs. 3% budgeted escalator in most corporate forecasts. Zero competitors (EnergyCAP, Arcadia, Schneider, Gridium, Nectar) offer PUC docket monitoring as a product feature — confirmed white space.

Deep Secondary Research adds four quantified macro headwinds that make regulatory intelligence urgent rather than optional: (1) PJM capacity market surge — PJM capacity prices spiked from approximately $10,000/MW in 2024 to a projected $120,000/MW by the 2027/28 delivery year, a 12× increase that translates to approximately $110,000 of unbudgeted annual capacity cost per megawatt of peak demand for any commercial facility in the PJM footprint (Pennsylvania, Ohio, Maryland, New Jersey, Virginia, and parts of 10 other states). (2) Rate increase breadth — 97.5% of commercial facilities in the U.S. experienced at least one electric rate increase between 2020 and 2025; 71% of those rate increases outpaced general inflation over the same period. (3) ERCOT wholesale volatility — ERCOT wholesale power prices rose approximately 45% from 2025 to 2026, driven by data center load growth in the DFW and Austin corridors, directly relevant to the Texas-first beachhead strategy. (4) State-level severity — New Jersey commercial rates increased 9.1% in 2024–2025; Rhode Island increased 8.6%; California's commercial rate schedule increased 7.3% — all above the 3% corporate budget escalator, creating a documented budget gap for any customer that locked annual utility budgets before rates were finalized.

**Deliverable Link:** Product Module Recommendations; Engagement Trigger Map

**Strength: STRONG**

---

### Finding 6: The IT Security Gate Is a Real and Recurring Enterprise Adoption Barrier

**Primary Evidence (3 interviews):** Phil Combs (Trane): "You've got guys like me that would love to use this, and then IT says no, no, no." Trane internally restricts AI tools its own employees want to use, despite selling AI to clients. AdventHealth (Scott Sukits): BrainBox AI — delivered via Trane, already contracted — has been stuck in IT/cybersecurity review for 2.5+ years in Central Florida. The AdventHealth IT posture hardened significantly following the Johnson Controls ransomware attack, which hit the healthcare sector broadly and triggered enterprise-wide AI vendor review protocols; this context explains why the delay is measured in years, not months. Not a concern or a slow process: 2.5 years of completed security review with no end date. Scott Czubkowski (Ascension): SOC 2 compliance referenced as the primary threshold for IT sign-off; Peer AI (Constellation Energy) cleared it — making SOC 2 Type II the documented compliance path that converted a healthcare IT security gate into a signed contract.

**Secondary Corroboration:** Enterprise SaaS literature confirms this is the dominant dynamic across cloud software adoption, not an edge case. 76% of organizations are actively increasing SaaS security budgets (secondary research). Commercial utility data — billing histories, rate structures, PPA terms — is classified as competitively sensitive, making IT's concern rational, not obstructionist. SOC 2 Type II is the primary certification that satisfies IT gatekeepers in the enterprise energy buyer context.

Deep Secondary Research quantifies the security gate with four statistics that should be included in investor and sales materials: (1) Forrester Research: 94% of enterprise IT departments report veto power over SaaS procurement — a single IT department can kill a deal regardless of end-user demand; (2) 45% of enterprise buyers report having abandoned a SaaS deployment mid-cycle specifically because the vendor failed to meet security requirements; (3) vendors with SOC 2 Type II certification win at a 62% higher rate in competitive enterprise deals than vendors without it; (4) SOC 2 certification shortens sales cycles by 3.2 months on average and increases average deal size by $100K+. On the cost side: SOC 2 Type II typically costs $20K–$80K and takes 6–12 months to complete — material for a pre-revenue company but a one-time investment that clears the single largest adoption barrier in the target verticals.

**Action:** Clear Current should lead with SOC 2 Type II in all materials before it is asked for. The AdventHealth example (2.5-year delay) suggests this is not a checkbox — it needs to be a proactive part of the sales narrative. The Forrester 94% statistic makes it table stakes, not a differentiator.

**Deliverable Link:** Engagement Trigger Map; User Journey Maps

**Strength: STRONG**

---

### Finding 7: Excel Is the Real Incumbent for Sophisticated Energy Analysts

**Primary Evidence (4 interviews):** Phil Combs (Trane): Excel spreadsheets are the primary tool for day-to-day energy analysis — even within Trane's own technology portfolio. Specialized software is only used for guaranteed savings contracts. Roger Goldstein (Panda): NG Insight data extracted and pulled to Excel for scrubbing and year-over-year trend monitoring. Walt Taylor (Panda): described Excel-based exception management workflows as standard across his QSR client base. Troy Nevells (UT Austin): similar Excel-based reconciliation described from his Yale experience.

**Secondary Corroboration:** Verdantix 2023: all 15 leading EMS vendors incorporating AI because buyers want automation over manual spreadsheet workflows. EnergyCAP user reviews reference spreadsheet-based workarounds as common even among paying customers.

**Deliverable Link:** Product Module Recommendations; User Journey Maps (the bar for adoption is "faster and more insightful than a well-structured Excel workbook")

**Strength: STRONG**

---

### Finding 8: EnergyCAP Is the Incumbent in Higher Ed — With Specific, Exploitable Gaps

**Primary Evidence (3 interviews):** Texas State (Andee Chamberlain): active EnergyCAP users who described specific gaps around district energy metering, blending sub-meter data with utility data, and chargeback complexity. Andi Ault (UT Austin): uses a billing system with limited flexibility; confirmed EnergyCAP limitations from the finance perspective. Troy Nevells (UT Austin): aware of EnergyCAP from Yale experience; noted it is best suited to settings with high external utility invoice volume (which UT Austin's self-generation model minimizes).

**Secondary Corroboration:** 132 G2/Capterra reviews confirm: "outdated, un-intuitive, glorified spreadsheet"; chargebacks "hard to understand"; report customization refused; $50–75K/year for 1,500 meters via reseller. EnergyCAP's "Watts AI" is nascent, retrofit, and rides on top of a tree-view navigation metaphor — not a conversational interface. No AI-native competitor has published campus logos at CMU/UCSD scale.

GTM Part 4 Research and the EnergyCAP competitive brief add three pricing reference points that anchor Clear Current's own pricing strategy: (1) EnergyCAP's list pricing is $40–$52/meter/year for the core UtilityManagement product, with implementation fees and managed services layered on top; (2) Texas State University (a confirmed EnergyCAP customer from primary research) is paying approximately $50K–$75K/year for 1,500 meters through a reseller, implying a blended rate of $33–$50/meter/year after reseller margin; (3) University of New Mexico (Lobo Energy) is documented at $9,600/year — a substantially lower contract, suggesting EnergyCAP prices smaller institutions at deeply discounted rates. The practical implication: Clear Current can price at or above EnergyCAP on a per-meter basis in higher ed while offering meaningfully better conversational AI, without triggering sticker shock. On the competitive disruption window: EnergyCAP has been owned by Resurgens Technology Partners since March 2021 — a five-year hold at typical PE holding period. A Resurgens exit or recap in 2026–2027 would create a 6–12 month window during which customers reconsider contracts, integration roadmaps, and vendor relationships — the classic PE-backed SaaS disruption window.

**Deliverable Link:** Competitive Positioning; Opportunity Heatmap; Product Module Recommendations

**Strength: STRONG**

---

### Finding 9: Arcadia's Competitive Gap Is Structural — And the Data Access Problem Is Now Confirmed (Updated Finding)

**Primary Evidence (3 interviews):** Marc Spieler (NVIDIA): named Arcadia as the primary AI-native competitive threat; raised the "why aren't people just signing?" conversion gap question. Walt Taylor (Panda): confirmed the ENGIE Impact / Arcadia relationship — a commercially significant finding about the data access dynamics in the QSR vertical. ENGIE Impact manages all of Panda's utility data; the relationship between ENGIE Impact and Arcadia creates a data-layer dependency that a new entrant must navigate. Adam Zavadsky (ENGIE Impact): ENGIE's moat is the data — 20 years of client utility history — not the analytics. AI is being used internally at ENGIE for bill processing efficiency, not surfaced to clients.

**Secondary Corroboration:** Arcadia has zero documented higher education customers after 14+ months of enterprise sales. Arcadia's enterprise product is built on a data/API architecture for developers, not a workflow tool for energy managers. Arcadia is simultaneously selling new enterprise accounts while migrating existing customers off the legacy Urjanet/Arc platform — PM job postings confirm this is the primary PM responsibility. No natural language chat interface exists in Arcadia's product. Arcadia enterprise customer list (Cox Enterprises, Arconic, Iron Mountain, Adobe, Intuit) is entirely corporate Fortune 500 — no university, no hospital, no district energy reference. Arcadia's Trustpilot score is 2.6/5 across 1,159 reviews — driven largely by its consumer-facing Perch brand, which is pending spinout and separation from the enterprise product. Arcadia's total disclosed funding is $649M at a last reported $1.5B valuation; the 12–24 month migration window created by the Urjanet/Arc platform transition is the primary competitive window documented in secondary research.

ENGIE Impact OCR coverage is approximately 90% — meaning roughly 10% of commercial utility bills processed through ENGIE's system are not captured electronically. This gap concentrates at the franchisee tier and smaller regional utilities, which is precisely where QSR and multi-site retail operators have the highest location density. ENGIE uses Arcadia's Arc API feeds for electronic bill data, meaning ENGIE's bill intelligence moat is the historical client data custody, not a proprietary data infrastructure.

Trane completed its acquisition of BrainBox AI in January 2025. BrainBox builds AI-based HVAC/BAS optimization using real-time sensors and is not a utility bill intelligence platform. The correct competitive framing: when Clear Current identifies HVAC-driven energy drift, Trane/BrainBox is the resolution pathway — a referral endpoint, not a competitor.

**New Implication:** The ENGIE Impact / Arcadia data relationship in QSR creates a structural access problem for Clear Current in that vertical. In healthcare and higher education — where ENGIE Impact is less dominant as the primary bill processor — this barrier does not apply, which reinforces the priority of those verticals. The bolt-on analytics framing (Walt Taylor: "just bolt you on") is the right entry strategy in ENGIE-heavy accounts rather than direct displacement at contract renewal.

**Deliverable Link:** Competitive Positioning; Opportunity Heatmap; Product Module Recommendations

**Strength: STRONG**

---

### Finding 10: Healthcare Is a Stronger Beachhead Than Initially Documented (New — Not in Original Synthesis)

**Primary Evidence (8 interviews across 5 systems):** This finding emerges from the depth and consistency of healthcare interviews rather than any single quote. Across five health systems, the research team found: (1) massive scale — 36 to 55 hospitals per system, 1,000–2,000 bills processed daily at AdventHealth; (2) sophisticated energy management teams with dedicated budgets; (3) real billing error experiences with documented dollar amounts; (4) sophisticated use of third-party processors (ENGIE Impact, Get Choice, Peer AI / Constellation) that create either partnership opportunities or competitive positioning; (5) willingness to pay for platforms that reduce budget uncertainty and catch errors; and (6) IT security gate as the primary adoption barrier, not price or product fit.

Scott Czubkowski (Ascension): detailed polar vortex ROI story — one event cost $7.2M in three days; proactive gas nomination alerting is now worth several million annually. This is the clearest documented ROI case for weather-integrated regulatory intelligence in the entire research program.

Ann Walston (Bon Secours): described billing error recovery as rare and difficult — a pain with no current software solution in her stack.

Ross Snow (Intermountain): third party missed a 3.5× bill spike; internal accountant caught it. A sophisticated 14-person team with $14B of BAS data still has a gap in utility invoice anomaly detection.

**Secondary Corroboration:** Secondary research documented 660+ district energy systems in the U.S. concentrated in universities and hospitals. Healthcare is EnergyCAP's secondary vertical (higher ed is primary) — creating an opening for a challenger. No AI-native competitor has published hospital logos with specific billing intelligence use cases.

**Deliverable Link:** Opportunity Heatmap; Product Module Recommendations; GTM Beachhead Strategy

**Strength: STRONG (primary) / MODERATE (secondary — secondary did not originally cover healthcare depth)**

---

### Finding 11: The "Ghost Meter / Address Reconciliation" Problem Is Real in Healthcare M&A (New — Not in Original Synthesis)

**Primary Evidence (1 interview, specifically surfaced):** AdventHealth (John Culver): described the #1 automation wish as basic data accuracy — consumption occurring at one address while the bill is paid somewhere else. In a 55-hospital system in active M&A, mergers, acquisitions, new builds, and divestments constantly change the portfolio, and keeping billing addresses in sync with physical locations is a continuous, imperfect process. ENGIE Impact sometimes catches this mismatch; sometimes it doesn't, depending on what the utility sends.

**Secondary Corroboration:** No secondary source specifically addresses the ghost meter / address reconciliation problem in the M&A context — this is a primary-first finding.

**Deliverable Link:** Product Module Recommendations (meter-to-address reconciliation should be an explicit feature, not an edge case)

**Strength: EMERGING (primary only; awaiting secondary corroboration)**

---

### Finding 12: The Owner-Operator Structure in Hospitality Changes the Buyer (New — Not in Original Synthesis)

**Primary Evidence (1 interview):** Randy Dawes (Hyatt): corporate does not touch utility invoices; billing responsibility falls to individual property owners, asset managers, and management companies. Invoice automation (solving late arrivals and late fees) is the most clearly articulated pain. Metrics fragmentation (different units across properties) is the second. The right buyer is not the brand's corporate team — it is the asset manager or management company running multiple properties.

**Secondary Corroboration:** Secondary research on CRE buyer behavior identified property managers and asset managers as the primary energy decision-maker in multi-tenant or managed property contexts, consistent with Hyatt's owner-operator structure.

**Deliverable Link:** Opportunity Heatmap (hospitality should be scored against asset manager buyer, not brand corporate buyer)

**Strength: MODERATE**

---

### Finding 13: Higher Ed Buying Cycle Is Calendar-Driven and Predictable (Reinforced)

**Primary Evidence (4 interviews):** Andee Chamberlain (Texas State): January–February budget planning; March–May gas purchasing and budget approval; September 1 new fiscal year. Andi Ault (UT Austin): budget submitted in March before City of Austin finalizes rates — the exact regulatory timing gap. Troy Nevells (UT Austin): capital equipment cycle is inverse to operations schedule (cooling replaced in winter; heating replaced in summer). Intermountain Health (Ross Snow): two-layer model — optimization calendar is seasonal; capital replacement calendar is counter-seasonal.

**Secondary Corroboration:** University of Wisconsin RFP: $500K–$2M contract value issued September with October deadline. Part 4 research: higher ed Fall awareness, Winter budget proposals, Spring RFP, Summer execution.

**Deliverable Link:** Seasonal Energy Management Calendar; Engagement Trigger Map

**Strength: STRONG**

---

### Finding 14: ERCOT Deregulation Creates a Defensible Texas Moat (Reinforced)

**Primary Evidence (3 interviews):** Marc Spieler: deregulated markets create billing complexity that is difficult for competitors to replicate from Boston or DC. Walt Taylor (Panda): PSC rate case experience in a deregulated state — the complexity of managing REP relationships, overlapping billing cycles during supplier transitions, and hedging decisions is directly navigable for a Texas-based team. Don Johnson (Trane): El Paso Electric background; ERCOT's commercial customer dynamics well understood.

**Secondary Corroboration:** ERCOT: 100+ REPs, 87% commercial switching rate, overlapping billing cycles during supplier transitions. 18 deregulated states mapped in Part 4 — Texas has highest billing error exposure from supplier switching.

**Deliverable Link:** Opportunity Heatmap; GTM Sequencing

**Strength: STRONG**

---

### Finding 15: The Three GTM Tracks — Marc Spieler's Framework (New — Not in Original Synthesis)

**Primary Evidence (1 interview, strategic framework):** Marc Spieler (NVIDIA) identified three parallel GTM strategies that address different buyer types and distribution economics:

Track 1 — Direct to Enterprise: continue founder-led direct sales to large, complex organizations. Current approach. Highest friction, highest margin, right for pre-revenue stage.

Track 2 — Sell to Utilities: offer billing accuracy as a service to the utility itself. Value proposition: catching underbilling (lost revenue) and preventing overbilling (regulatory risk, customer service cost, potential legal exposure). Utilities have whole commercial/industrial customer management teams; C&I customers account for 80% of power consumption.

Track 3 — License to ERP / Billing Software: embed Clear Current's AI audit capability as a module inside existing ERP stacks — Oracle, SAP, Microsoft Dynamics, JD Edwards, QuickBooks. Removes direct sales burden; ERP vendor becomes distribution channel.

**Secondary Corroboration:** Part 4 research confirmed the land-and-expand motion as the right GTM strategy for a pre-revenue SaaS company in a category where buyers are skeptical. ERP distribution channel not explored in secondary research — this is a primary-first strategic finding.

Deep Secondary adds one material data point for the ERP track: SAP integration projects for mid-to-large commercial organizations typically require 12–24 months from contract to production — meaning any ERP-embedded module strategy has a long lead time, but also creates high switching costs once embedded. This makes Track 3 (ERP licensing) a 24–36 month horizon play, not a near-term revenue source. Marc Spieler's framing of Track 1 (direct enterprise) as the right pre-revenue approach is confirmed by secondary research — ERP licensing only makes sense after the product has proven itself in direct deployments.

**Deliverable Link:** GTM Strategy; Investor Readiness Narrative

**Strength: EMERGING (primary first; secondary adds ERP timeline context)**

---

### Finding 16: The Property Tax Protest Pricing Model Is a Better Analogy Than SaaS Subscription (New — Not in Original Synthesis)

**Primary Evidence (1 interview, pricing framework):** Marc Spieler (NVIDIA): a property tax protest service operates on an evergreen agreement with a 50% savings share. Every year, the firm protests the tax increase and invoices 50% of the savings. If they don't win, the customer pays nothing. The model works because it is automated, recurring, low-friction, and the value is proven every cycle. Marc also described the savings-sharing trap: as soon as recoveries become large, clients push to renegotiate to a fixed fee. The right structure is a low flat monthly fee plus a percentage of identified savings — the fee creates a revenue floor and avoids the renegotiation dynamic.

**Secondary Corroboration:** Part 4 research confirmed willingness-to-pay dynamics in the enterprise energy buyer market, and GTM Part 4 adds substantial secondary data validating the pricing model and anchoring it with real numbers.

GTM Part 4 quantifies the pricing landscape across four dimensions: (1) **Pilot proof point** — across 12 beta/pilot deployments in comparable utility bill intelligence platforms, the median identified error pool was $10M — meaning a typical pilot produces $10M in identifiable billing errors, of which a percentage is recoverable. This is the primary investor proof point: the product finds money, which makes the pricing conversation straightforward. (2) **Contingency fee benchmarks** — the contingency fee model is well-established in utility audit services: standard range is 20–50% of recovered overcharges; UtiliSave, the most documented specialist, charges 40%; property tax protest services (Marc Spieler's analogy) typically charge 30–50%. A 20–30% contingency rate positions Clear Current at the lower-cost end of the market while remaining highly credible. (3) **Pricing ladder** — GTM Part 4 documents a three-tier structure benchmarked against competitive platforms: an Essentials tier (basic invoice ingestion and anomaly alerts) at $25–$50/meter/year; an Optimize tier (regulatory intelligence, trend analysis, conversational Q&A) at $75–$100/meter/year; and an Enterprise Platform tier (API access, custom reporting, dedicated CSM) at $120K–$250K/year flat. (4) **ROI benchmarks** — energy management software deployments in the secondary literature produce an average of 7.5% savings on total utility spend, with a median payback period of 2.6 years; at $500K annual utility spend (small healthcare or QSR portfolio), that is $37,500/year in savings, making the willingness-to-pay range of $15K–$40K/year rational and sustainable.

**Note on the property tax protest model:** The structural insight from Marc Spieler is confirmed by secondary pricing research — the flat-fee-plus-contingency structure (low monthly platform fee + % of identified savings) creates a revenue floor while avoiding the renegotiation dynamic that pure contingency arrangements trigger once savings become large and predictable.

**Deliverable Link:** Product Module Recommendations; Investor Readiness Narrative

**Strength: MODERATE (primary framework strong; secondary adds specific price anchors)**

---

## 04 — The Five Findings That Should Shape Every Deliverable

These are the insights that appear in both primary and secondary research across multiple independent interviews with enough consistency and specificity to anchor every deliverable.

---

**FINDING 1 — The Reactive Buyer Is Universal Across Verticals**

Eleven of sixteen interviews confirmed that commercial energy buyers operate reactively. The pattern holds across higher education (Texas State, UT Austin), healthcare (Bon Secours, Houston Methodist, AdventHealth), QSR (Panda), and advisory (ENGIE Impact, JLL). The only exception is Intermountain Health, which operates an unusually sophisticated 14-person proactive team — and even they missed a $40K-to-$140K spike via their third-party processor. EnergyCAP's own 2026 survey found 76% of buyers reactive despite knowing costs will rise. The product must surface insights proactively. The push dashboard layer is not optional.

---

**FINDING 2 — The Dual-Audience Problem Is Structural, Documented by Both Verticals, and Unsolved by Any Competitor**

Six interviews across higher education and healthcare confirmed the finance/operations split from multiple chairs. Andee Chamberlain described it from operations; Andi Ault confirmed it from finance. Ann Walston (Bon Secours), Scott Czubkowski (Ascension), Troy Nevells, and the AdventHealth team all described versions of the same split. No current tool — EnergyCAP, Arcadia, Peer AI — serves both audiences from a single platform. This is Clear Current's clearest product differentiation opportunity.

---

**FINDING 3 — Billing Errors Are Government-Documented, Not Vendor Folklore, and Threshold Systems Miss the Worst Ones**

Seven interviews produced specific, named billing error examples — not anecdotes but specific incidents with dollar amounts. The most important new finding: threshold-based exception systems (NG Insight at Panda, Peer AI's predecessor at Ascension) miss gradual and systematic errors that establish a new baseline. FERC confirmed $108M mischarged at a single utility; Rhode Island's PUC found 83% of flagged exceptions sent to customers anyway. This framing — regulatory enforcement, not vendor marketing — is how Clear Current should position the billing error opportunity in every investor and customer conversation.

---

**FINDING 4 — Regulatory Intelligence Is White Space With a Quantified Business Case Across Two Verticals**

Five interviews produced specific, independent evidence for the regulatory intelligence use case: Phil Combs called it the "killer feature nobody has"; Andi Ault's March budget / July rate decision is the archetypal use case; Walt Taylor has actually won a PSC rate case and understands the process deeply; Ann Walston identified rate trend visibility as the second proactive lever her finance team lacks; Sean Sevy acknowledged the gap explicitly. Zero competitors offer PUC docket monitoring. Scott Czubkowski's polar vortex story provides the healthcare-specific version — weather-triggered energy procurement alerting estimated at several million dollars annually for a single health system.

---

**FINDING 5 — The Right Beachhead Is Healthcare First, Higher Ed Second — Not the Reverse**

The original synthesis prioritized higher education based on the first five interviews. With eight interviews across five healthcare systems, a stronger picture has emerged. Healthcare systems — 36 to 55 hospitals per organization, $265M annual utility spend at Ascension, 1,000–2,000 daily bills at AdventHealth — represent larger contracts, more sophisticated buyers, and a less fortified incumbent position than higher education. EnergyCAP's higher ed moat (campus chargebacks, district energy) is real and documented. Healthcare is EnergyCAP's secondary vertical — the beachhead is cleaner. The IT security gate applies in both verticals but healthcare IT is better resourced to clear it quickly when the value case is made clearly.

---

## 05 — Market Sizing, Investor Benchmarks & Comparable Transactions

This section compiles secondary data relevant to Clear Current's investor narrative — market size, Series A thresholds, and comparable M&A transactions in the energy intelligence and facility management SaaS space. These data points were not tied to individual primary interviews but are directly relevant to fundraising positioning.

---

### Series A Benchmarks for B2B SaaS (GTM Part 4, Thread 2)

GTM Part 4 secondary research surfaced the following Series A medians for enterprise B2B SaaS companies in adjacent verticals (energy tech, facility management, sustainability software):

| Metric | Series A Median | Range |
|---|---|---|
| ARR at raise | $1.5M–$2.0M | $800K–$4M |
| Net Revenue Retention (NRR) | 101–104% | 95–115% |
| Burn multiple | 1.6× | 1.2×–2.5× |
| Gross margin | 68–72% | 60–80% |
| Customer count | 15–35 logos | 8–60 |
| Lead investor check | $6M–$10M | $4M–$15M |

The $10M identified-error proof point from the 12-pilot benchmark is the most investable metric in Clear Current's current story — it directly answers the investor's core question ("does the product find money?") with a specific, secondary-corroborated number.

---

### Comparable M&A Transactions — Energy Intelligence & Facility SaaS

| Company | Acquirer | Year | Revenue at Exit | Exit Multiple | Key Insight |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|
| Brightly Software (formerly Dude Solutions) | Siemens | 2022 | ~$160M ARR | ~9.8× ARR ($1.575B) | Facility management SaaS; highest comp in space |
| EnerNOC | Enel | 2018 | ~$200M revenue | 1.5× revenue | Demand response + analytics; pre-AI |
| Lucid (BuildingOS) | Verdigris | 2019 | ~$10M ARR | ~6× ARR | Energy monitoring SaaS; early AI |
| Urjanet | Arcadia | 2022 | Undisclosed | Strategic | Bill data API layer; now inside Arc platform; Arcadia total raised $649M post-acquisition |
| Wattics | EnergyCAP | 2022 | ~€3M ARR (est.) | Strategic | Interval data analytics; Dublin |
| Carbon Lighthouse | Hines | 2021 | ~$15M revenue | Strategic | Building efficiency; CRE-focused |

The Brightly transaction at 9.8× ARR is the ceiling comp for a well-positioned facility/energy SaaS business with scale. The Arcadia/Urjanet deal is the closest structural analog to Clear Current's data-and-AI positioning — and Arcadia subsequently raised $649M (last valuation $1.5B) and serves 1.35M accounts, establishing the scale ceiling for the category. **Note:** earlier drafts of this synthesis used $566M; the $649M figure reflects the most recent disclosed funding total as used in the D6 Competitive Intelligence Brief and should be treated as current until Arcadia's next public disclosure.

---

### Market Size — Secondary Research Summary

| Segment | Market Size (2025) | CAGR | Source |
|---|---|---|---|
| Global utility bill management software | $2.1B | 12.4% | MarketsandMarkets 2025 |
| Energy management software (enterprise) | $8.6B | 14.2% | Grand View Research 2025 |
| Utility analytics & AI | $4.3B | 18.7% | IDC 2025 |
| U.S. commercial electricity spend (annual) | $241B | 5.9% | EIA CBECS 2018, projected forward |
| U.S. commercial energy spend — all fuels (annual) | $380B | 5.9% | EIA 2025 (electricity + gas + other) |
| Addressable error pool (1-in-5 bills × avg commercial spend) | ~$11–19B | — | Derived from EIA electricity spend + EnergyCAP/UtiliSave error rates |

**TAM note for investor materials:** The $241B figure (electricity-only, EIA CBECS 2018 projected at 5.9% CAGR) is the anchor used in the investor narrative — it is the more conservative and auditable number. The $380B figure covers all commercial energy including natural gas and other fuels; both are accurate but serve different framing purposes. Use $241B as the primary investor TAM and clarify the scope if challenged.

**BEMS market penetration:** Only 4% of U.S. commercial buildings had a Building Energy Management System as of the most recent EIA Commercial Buildings Energy Consumption Survey (CBECS). This penetration figure — from a government source — is the most credible proof of how early the commercial energy software category is, and belongs in the investor narrative's "why now" beat.

**Demand charge context (NREL):** NREL analysis documents that demand charges represent 30–70% of a typical commercial electricity bill. A practical example from NREL: a commercial customer hitting a 1,000 kW summer peak under an 80% ratchet clause locks approximately $144,000/year in demand charges regardless of subsequent usage — illustrating why ratchet detection and demand charge explanation are high-value product features. This government-sourced math replaces any vendor claim about billing error magnitude in investor and sales materials.

---

### Serviceable Obtainable Market — 24-Month Targets (Aligned to D7 Investor Narrative)

| Segment | Target Accounts | ACV Range | 24-Month Revenue |
|---|---|---|---|
| Higher education (beachhead) | 10–15 | $30K–$75K | $300K–$1.1M |
| Healthcare systems | 3–5 | $75K–$150K | $225K–$750K |
| Multi-site commercial / QSR (ERCOT-first) | 5–10 | $25K–$50K | $125K–$500K |
| **Total SOM (24-month)** | **18–30** | — | **$650K–$2.4M** |

The SOM range of $650K–$2.4M across 18–30 accounts represents the realistic fundraise narrative for a Series A raise in 2026, consistent with the $1.5–2.0M ARR median documented for comparable B2B SaaS raises in adjacent verticals.

---

### CRE Vertical — Competitive Data

| Platform | Scale / Backing | Primary Strength | Relevance to CC |
|---|---|---|---|
| WatchWire (Tango) | 500+ CRE customers; Berkshire-backed via 2023 acquisition | GRESB, CDP, GRI reporting; building performance standards | CRE sustainability/reporting lead; limited bill-intelligence depth |
| Measurabl | Free tier onboarded 11,000+ buildings in 4 weeks (2024) | ESG data collection; GRESB workflow automation | ESG data aggregation at scale; not a tariff-truth or NL bill-Q&A platform |
| JLL Technologies | Internal AI energy-management and utility-bill intelligence build in active development | Custodied client data across global Fortune 200 portfolio | Most significant CRE competitive signal — enterprise CRE buyer may "build first" not "buy first" |

**JLL Technologies internal-build flag:** Jamare Bates (JLL, primary interview) described JLL Technologies as actively building internal AI energy-management and utility-bill intelligence tooling on data they already hold across their client portfolio. This is the most significant finding for CRE vertical positioning: the enterprise CRE buyer in this segment may already be building the product Clear Current would sell them. CRE beachhead strategy should lean toward partnership or licensing rather than direct SaaS displacement until the JLL Technologies roadmap is clearer. This finding was not captured in original secondary competitive research and is primary-research-first.

**GRESB 2025 scale:** GRESB engaged 1,002 fund managers and processed 2,382 assessments in 2025 — confirming the ESG reporting compliance demand in CRE is real and growing, even if the primary buyer for Clear Current in that vertical is more complex than initially modeled.

---

## 06 — Research Gaps Identified

The following areas lack sufficient primary or secondary coverage to support strong claims:

**Commercial Real Estate Direct Buyer Voice** — No primary interview completed with a REIT energy manager, property manager, or CRE operator. Jamare Bates (JLL) provided the advisory perspective. D5 heatmap scores CRE lowest on buyer readiness (2/5) partly for this reason. Additionally, JLL Technologies is actively building internal AI energy-management and utility-bill tooling on custodied client data — a finding from the Jamare Bates primary interview that materially changes the CRE GTM thesis. CRE beachhead strategy should lean toward partnership/licensing until the JLL Technologies internal-build roadmap is clearer.

**Hospitality Direct Buyer Voice** — Randy Dawes (Hyatt) confirmed the owner-operator structure shifts the real buyer to asset managers and management companies — but no interview was completed with that actual buyer. D5 heatmap scores hospitality at 13/25 (lowest) and explicitly flags "No primary interview captured for this vertical yet." NYC Local Law 97 (annual GHG emissions report due May 1 for buildings over 25,000 sq ft, with fines beginning 2024) is a documented CRE/hospitality compliance trigger with no primary voice of customer behind it. This trigger is catalogued in D1 (REG-04) based on secondary research only.

**Sewer/Water Overbilling** — Walt Taylor (Panda) flagged that most QSR operators have no meter for how much water is actually leaving the restaurant — creating a structural blind spot for sewer/wastewater billing errors that compound water charges. D6 flags this as "EMERGING — one primary, no secondary." No secondary research on commercial sewer/water billing error rates or detection methods.

**Water Billing as a Parallel Use Case** — Andee Chamberlain (Texas State) flagged water as increasingly complex and expensive; UT Austin operates its own wells. No secondary research on commercial water billing error rates.

**Multi-Family / Apartment Billing** — Not covered in primary or secondary research. Billee Technologies ($9.15M seed, July 2025) is active here per secondary research.

**Utility-Side Buyer Persona** — Marc Spieler identified utilities as a potential second GTM track (D7 Beat 3). No primary interview with a utility commercial/industrial customer management team; no secondary research on utility-side procurement software spend.

**ERP Integration Economics** — Marc Spieler identified licensing to ERP platforms as a viable third GTM track. SAP integration timelines run 12–24 months making this a 24–36 month horizon play. No secondary research on ERP marketplace economics or billing software integration commission structures.

**Willingness-to-Pay Primary Validation** — No primary interview produced a named dollar figure a buyer would actually commit to paying for a standalone analytics layer. All pricing validation in Section 05 is secondary benchmark-derived. Two interviews with a healthcare CFO or QSR energy director on actual WTP would convert benchmarks into validated anchors.

**Win/Loss Data** — No interviews with organizations that bought EnergyCAP, Arcadia, or Peer AI and left, or bought and regretted it. This perspective typically produces the most honest feature gap and switching cost intelligence and is the most actionable gap for the next research sprint.

---
